   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d57601396" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Anode-free sodium metal batteries (AFSMBs) are regarded as the “ceiling” for current sodium-based batteries. However, their practical application is hindered by the unstable electrolyte and interfacial chemistry at the high-voltage cathode and anode-free side, especially under extreme temperature conditions. Here, an advanced electrolyte design strategy based on electrolyte solvation engineering is presented, which shapes a weakly solvating anion-stabilized (WSAS) electrolyte by balancing the interaction between the Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent and Na<sup>+</sup>-anion. The special interaction constructs rich contact ion pairs (CIPs) /aggregates (AGGs) clusters at the electrode/electrolyte interface during the dynamic solvation process which facilitates the formation of a uniform and stable interfacial layer, enabling highly stable cycling of 4.0&nbsp;V-class layered oxide cathode from −40&nbsp;°C to 60&nbsp;°C and excellent reversibility of Na plating/stripping with an ultrahigh average CE of 99.89%. Ultimately, industrial multi-layer anode-free pouch cells using the WSAS electrolyte achieve 80% capacity remaining after 50 cycles and even deliver 74.3% capacity at −30&nbsp;°C. This work takes a pivotal step for the further development of high-energy-density Na batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202410261-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202410261-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been regarded as a promising energy storage technology to supplement the existing lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) for most applications owing to their suitable energy density, environmentally friendly, simplified structure, and safety.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0001_R_d57601388e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - ACS Energy Lett. ACS Energy Lett. Nat. Energy">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, most of the SIBs based on a Na<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;intercalation/deintercalation anode (e.g., hard carbon) only deliver cell-level specific energy of ≈160&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>, which is 40% less than that of commercial LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0002_R_d57601388e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Nat. Rev. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared to the conventional SIBs configuration, AFSMBs with the removal of the anode-active materials will maximize the gravimetric and volumetric energy densities of batteries, even up to 300&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> and 680&nbsp;Wh L.<sup>−1[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0003_R_d57601388e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - iScience">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, the AFSMBs always show a poor cycle life due to there is no excess sodium in the anode that can be used to replenish the loss of active sodium, which significantly hinders the practical application of high-energy density AFSMBs for the large-scale power and energy storage market.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0004_R_d57601388e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Batteries Mater. Today">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To improve the performance of AFSMBs, it is vital to recognize the reasons for the loss of limited Na inventory. First, for the anode-free side, a high overpotential of Na plating on the current collector easily causes the uneven deposition of Na metal, exacerbating the side effects between the Na metal and the electrolyte during the plating/striping process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0005_R_d57601388e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, it is difficult to form a stable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) on the highly reactive Na metal surfaces, leading to a continuous decomposition of electrolytes and a rapid loss of Na inventory.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0006_R_d57601388e597" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Rev.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To address the above issues, reliable solutions have been proposed by modifying the current collectors to regulate sodium deposition,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0007_R_d57601388e602" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> but it always requires some complicated process and high cost. In contrast, the electrolyte modifications are much simpler and easier to implement for the AFSMBs, which not only improves the behavior of Na deposition but also enhances the stability of SEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0008_R_d57601388e608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the previous report, the ether-based electrolyte has been proven to achieve high reversibility for the Na plating on Cu, which is attributed to the ether solvent in comparison with the traditional carbonate solvent, having higher reductive stability and better compatibility with Na metal by constructing a thin and robust SEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0009_R_d57601388e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Cent. Sci. Energy Storage Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the ether-based electrolyte is difficult to match the high-voltage (≥ 4.0&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) sodium cathodes owing to its inferior antioxidation ability. Specifically, the electrolyte is susceptible to oxidative owing to the high oxidation state of Ni<sup>4+</sup> from the NFM cathode at high potential and then exacerbates the accumulation of by-products within the cathode electrolyte interface (CEI), leading to the formation of thick CEI which hinders Na<sup>+</sup> transport and intercalation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0010_R_d57601388e625" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nat. Energy">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, there is an urgent need to design an ideal electrolyte system that not only stabilizes the cathode/electrolyte interface at high potential but also improves the reversibility of sodium metal plating/stripping on the anode-free side.</p>
         
         <p>To address the thorny issues of AFSMBs, some electrolyte strategies have been reported in recent years, mainly focusing on the modification of diglyme (G2)-based electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0011_R_d57601388e633" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, small amounts of functional NaBF<sub>4</sub> are added to the G2-based electrolytes to form the B-containing species in both the CEI and SEI, which contributes to enhancing the stability of the electrode/electrolyte interface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0012_R_d57601388e640" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Energy">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, regulating the coordination relation of Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent by refining the volume ratio of G2 and tetraethylene glycol dimethyl ether (G4) can facilitate the Na metal plate on the current collector and be compatible with high-voltage P2/O3-layered cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0013_R_d57601388e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the multiple ether bonds of diglyme molecules render strong chelation with Na,<sup>+[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0014_R_d57601388e653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> likely resulting in a decrease in the movement of Na<sup>+</sup> and an increase in the desolvation energy of Na<sup>+</sup>, especially at low temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0015_R_d57601388e663" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Mater. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the ether co-solvent with single or double ether bonds, such as tetrahydrofuran (THF), 2-methyl tetrahydrofuran (MeTHF), and 1,2-dimethoxyethane (DME), can reduce the desolvation energy and show high ion transfer ability, enabling the reversibility of Na plating/stripping at ultralow temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0016_R_d57601388e668" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Storage Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, they are only compatible with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> which has a low capacity and operation voltage (≤3.8&nbsp;V), which significantly limits the energy density of AFSMBs. In contrast, the Na-based layered oxide cathode (Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>, TM = transition metal) has been recognized as a promising commercial Na cathode material due to its high specific capacity, simple synthesis processes, and low cost, especially for the 4.0&nbsp;V-class Na(Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.33</sub>)O<sub>2</sub> (NFM),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0017_R_d57601388e695" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Angew.Chem. Int. Ed. Nat. Commun. Nat. Chem.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which is an appropriate choice for increasing the energy density of AFSMBs. Moreover, it is a reliable method to extend the antioxidative window by increasing the ratio of salt to solvents or reducing the solvating power of solvents to enable the formation of a stable anion-derived interfacial layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0018_R_d57601388e701" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces. Nat. Energy Angew.Chem. Int. Ed. ACS Energy Lett.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, it may still cause an unstable interfacial layer at the electrode surface owing to blindly changing the concentration or solvent to only regulate the solvation structure in the bulk environment but ignore the interfacial solvation process, leading to the battery performance degradation. Therefore, tailoring the dynamic solvation behavior of electrolytes at the electrode/electrolyte interface to form a robust and stable interfacial layer is the key to achieving the state-of-the-art performance of AFSMBs under extreme temperatures.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we propose an advanced electrolyte design strategy from the electrolyte solvation engineering based on the dynamic evolution of Na<sup>+</sup> solvation structure, and thus a WSAS electrolyte (e.g., 2.0&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in 1, 2-diethoxyethane (DEE)) is successfully prepared by balancing the interaction between the Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent and Na<sup>+</sup>-anion. In the WSAS electrolyte, the anion-participated clutters (i.e., CIPs/AGGs) not only are constructed in the bulk environment but also still enriched at the electrode/electrolyte interface during the cycling process, facilitating the formation of inorganic-rich CEI and SEI, contributing to the superior electrochemical performance of anode-free battery. Consequently, the electrolyte solvation regulating strategy will provide insight into the practical exploration of other potential ether-based solvents for ultrahigh-energy density anode-free batteries in the future.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202410261-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202410261-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410261-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202410261-sec-0030-title">2.1 Design Principles and Solvation Structure of Electrolyte</h3>
            
            <p>In the traditional electrolyte, the cations (e.g., Na<sup>+</sup>) are coordinated by solvent molecules or anions. Among them, solvents are mainly divided into strongly solvating or weakly solvating solvents, which can regulate the ion-dipole and ion-ion interaction (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0001">1a</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0019_R_d57601388e739" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Acc. Chem. Res.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the case of a normal concentration electrolyte (e.g.,1.0&nbsp;M), the use of a strongly solvating solvent leads to the formation of an electrolyte with a solvent-dominated inner solvation structure, termed a strongly solvating solvent-dominated (SSSD) electrolyte. Conversely, when a weakly solvating solvent is employed as the main solvent, the electrolyte induces anions to participate in coordination, forming a weakly solvating anion-participated (WSAP) electrolyte. A simple and effective way to improve the electrochemical stability of electrolytes is by increasing the salt concentration. The anions always tend to enter the inner solvation structure with concentration increases, but the anions will continue to disturb due to their competitive interaction between Na<sup>+</sup>-anion and Na<sup>+</sup>-strongly solvating solvents, resulting in the formation of a strongly solvating anion-disturbed (SSAD) electrolyte. In contrast, weakly solvating solvent reduces the Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent interaction and then constructs a stable interaction between Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent and Na<sup>+</sup>-anions, especially when the Na<sup>+</sup>-anion interaction is enhanced as the salt ratio increases. This balance between anion and solvent coordination enables the anions to remain within the inner solvation structure, creating a weakly solvating anion-stabilized (WSAS) electrolyte.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202410261-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/464c4b4a-a0d7-4329-83bc-055c7ff253f5/adma202410261-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/464c4b4a-a0d7-4329-83bc-055c7ff253f5/adma202410261-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/81b2429a-991e-486e-899b-fd07cb2925ea/adma202410261-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/464c4b4a-a0d7-4329-83bc-055c7ff253f5/adma202410261-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410261-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410261" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Design principles and solvation structure of WSAS electrolyte. a) Solvation designing illustration of different electrolytes. Spatial distribution functions (SDFs) of Na<sup>+</sup> and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> around b) DME or c) DEE solvent. d) <sup>23</sup>Na and e) <sup>19</sup>F-NMR spectra of various concentrations of DEE or DME-based electrolytes. Radius distribution functions (RDF) of 2&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in f) DME and g) DEE electrolytes. Percentage distribution of possible coordinated clusters around Na<sup>+</sup> of 2&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in h) DME (i.e., SSAD) and i) DEE (i.e., WSAS) electrolytes from MD simulation.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To better demonstrate the feasibility of the design principles discussed above, DME and DEE solvents are selected as verification examples for electrolyte design. First, spatial distribution functions (SDFs) are employed to analyze the spatial distribution of different coordinated components around the solvent. In the case of DME, Na⁺ is closely associated with the solvent, while PF<sub>6</sub>⁻ is more dispersed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0001">1b</a>), classifying DME as a strongly solvating solvent. In contrast, both Na<sup>+</sup> and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> are tightly associated with DEE, indicating that DEE acts as a weakly solvating solvent (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0001">1c</a>). Such differences may stem from the different steric hindrance and dielectric constants of DME and DEE.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0020_R_d57601388e810" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small J. Am. Chem. Soc.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) analyses were conducted on electrolytes based on different concentrations of DME and DEE to examine their solvation structures. As the salt concentration increased from 1&nbsp;M to 2&nbsp;M, the <sup>23</sup>Na and <sup>19</sup>F-NMR peaks of both DME- and DEE-based electrolytes shifted upfield (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0001">1d,e</a>), indicating that Na<sup>+</sup>-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> interactions were strengthened, and ion pairing increased at higher concentrations. Regardless of salt concentration, the <sup>23</sup>Na and <sup>19</sup>F-NMR peaks of DEE-based electrolytes exhibited a greater upfield shift compared to those of DME-based electrolytes, suggesting stronger Na⁺-PF₆⁻ interactions in DEE-based electrolytes due to the weaker solvation ability of the DEE solvent.</p>
            
            <p>To understand the solvation structure of electrolytes with strong ion-ion interactions, radial distribution functions (RDF, g (r)) and corresponding coordination numbers (N(r)) of 2&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME and DEE electrolytes were analyzed using molecular dynamics (MD) simulation (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). In the case of 2&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME, the initial peaks of Na<span class="icomoon"></span>O (from the C<span class="icomoon"></span>O of the solvent) and Na-F (from PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>) appear at approximately 0.23 Å, with the coordination number of DME being 1.50, higher than that of the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> (i.e., 0.67) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0001">1f</a>), indicating that the solvent dominates the inner solvation structure. In contrast, for 2&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DEE, while the initial peaks of Na-O and Na-F also appear at about 0.23 Å, the coordination number of DEE solvent is 0.68, lower than that of the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> (1.28) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0001">1g</a>), suggesting that the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anion more readily participates in the inner solvation layer. Meanwhile, the percentage distribution of possible coordinated clusters around Na<sup>+</sup> also provides further insight into solvation structure. In the 2&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME electrolyte, the dominant solvated clusters are Na<sup>+</sup>−2DME-2PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup>−2DME-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, and Na<sup>+</sup>−3DME, accounting for 55.3%, 29.9%, and 11.4%, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0001">1h</a> and Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). This result suggests that, despite its high concentration, DME-based electrolytes maintain solvent-dominated solvation environments due to the strong solvating ability of DME, leading to competitive interactions between Na<sup>+</sup>-DME and Na<sup>+</sup>-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and the disturbance of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions. Therefore, the 2&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME electrolyte is classified as an SSAD electrolyte. When DEE is used as the solvent, the primary solvated clusters shift to Na<sup>+</sup>-DEE-3PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup>−2DEE-2PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, and Na<sup>+</sup>-DEE-2PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, representing 55.3%, 29.9% and 11.4%, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0001">1i</a> and Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), indicating a stable interaction between Na<sup>+</sup>-DEE and Na<sup>+</sup>-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, which allows PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> to remain firmly in the solvation structure. Overall, the simulation results confirm that the 2&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DEE electrolyte can be classified as a WSAS electrolyte.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410261-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202410261-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance of 4.0&nbsp;V-Class Cathode with Various Electrolytes</h3>
            
            <p>A high antioxidation capability is crucial for assembling high-energy batteries with high-voltage cathodes, such as the 4.0&nbsp;V-class Na(Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>)O<sub>2</sub> (NFM). As demonstrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0002">2a</a>, the Na||NFM cell used WSAS electrolyte maintains a capacity retention of 86.8% after 100 cycles at 1.0 C with a high mass loading of 4.5&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. In contrast, the cell using SSAD electrolyte shows a reduced capacity retention of 79.6%. For the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathode, there is no significant difference in the cycling performance of Na||NVP cells with the aforementioned electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). This suggests that enhancing the compatibility of 4.0V-class NFM with the electrolyte is more challenging. Moreover, the low-concentration WSAP (i.e., 1.0&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DEE) electrolyte also shows a higher capacity retention of 83.8% compared to SSSD (i.e., 1.0&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME, 74.3%) and Carbonate-based electrolyte (72.9%), and increasing the electrolyte concentration, such as using 2.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DEE, does not significantly improve capacity retention but instead leads to higher costs (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Meanwhile, the typical voltage profiles of the NFM cathode in the WSAS electrolyte exhibit slower increases in electrochemical polarization and a smaller capacity decrease over 100 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0002">2b</a>). Moreover, the distribution of relaxation times (DRT)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0021_R_d57601388e990" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Joule">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> analysis, derived from electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) data of the cycled Na||NFM cell, reveals that the anode interfacial resistance (R<sub>SEI</sub>), cathode interfacial resistance (R<sub>CEI</sub>), and charge transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>) of the cell with WSAS electrolyte are lower than those of the SSAD electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). This suggests that the WSAS electrolyte is better suited for the 4.0&nbsp;V-class NFM, suppressing electrolyte decomposition, which is further evidenced by the low oxidation current of cycled NFM in WSAS electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, the WSAS electrolyte displays superior rate performance during high-current cycling, delivering a specific capacity of 94.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> under 10 C (i.e., 1400&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>), which surpasses the 79.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> achieved with SSAD electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0002">2c</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202410261-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1b3e5f77-eb43-42b0-b514-9f6506e38285/adma202410261-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1b3e5f77-eb43-42b0-b514-9f6506e38285/adma202410261-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/780c2d12-c566-4359-b7ea-1dd937369672/adma202410261-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1b3e5f77-eb43-42b0-b514-9f6506e38285/adma202410261-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410261-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410261" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties of Na||NFM cell with WSAS and SSAD electrolytes. a) cycling performance and b) corresponding voltage profiles of the cell with high mass loading of NFM cathode (4.5&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>) at 1.0 C. c) Rate capacity of the cell from 1.0 C to 10 C. Cycling stability of the cell at d) high temperature (60&nbsp;°C) and e) different low temperatures (from 10&nbsp;°C to −40&nbsp;°C).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Furthermore, the WSAS electrolytes also exhibit an available physicochemical property (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, to extend performance across a wide temperature range, the ionic conductivity-temperature dependence of various electrolytes was measured from 60&nbsp;°C to −40&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> and Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The corresponding data of WSAS electrolyte is fitted by the Arrhenius equation but that of SSAD electrolyte is difficult to achieve owing to its low freezing point (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), making it unsuitable for operation at low temperatures. At elevated temperatures (60&nbsp;°C), the cell with WSAS electrolyte retains a capacity retention of 92.1% after 50 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0002">2d</a>). The temperature-dependent capacity presents a decreased tendency as the electrolyte freezes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0002">2e</a>), especially when the cell using SSAD electrolyte can no longer operate when the temperature is below 0&nbsp;°C. In comparison, the cell using WSAS electrolyte shows excellent temperature adaptability, delivering a specific capacity of 68.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at −40&nbsp;°C (53.3% of its room-temperature capacity) and achieving a capacity retention of 78.0% after 100 cycles at −20&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). Based on the above experiences and measurements, although the WSAS electrolyte uses ether as the main solvent, it is conducive to promoting the formation of a stable cathode/electrolyte interface and is well-compatible with a 4.0&nbsp;V-class layered oxide cathode, even in extreme temperatures.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410261-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202410261-sec-0050-title">2.3 Dynamic Evolution of Cathode/Electrolyte Interface</h3>
            
            <p>To obtain the key information about the interface evolution process, the in situ FTIR was used to explore the dynamic distribution of various solvation clusters (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). The results show that the C<span class="icomoon"></span>O<span class="icomoon"></span>C stretching of free DME is located at 1105 cm<sup>−1</sup>, while the C<span class="icomoon"></span>O<span class="icomoon"></span>C group coordinated to Na<sup>+</sup> has a redshift to 1082 cm<sup>−1</sup> for Na<sup>+</sup>-DME, as confirmed by ex situ FTIR analysis (upper part of <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0003">3a</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0018_R_d57601388e1093" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces. Nat. Energy Angew.Chem. Int. Ed. ACS Energy Lett.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the intensity of Na<sup>+</sup>-DME increases as the salt ratio in the SSAD electrolyte rises. From the differential in-situ FTIR contour map (lower part of Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0003">3a</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a,b</a>, Supporting Information), the intensity of both free DME and Na<sup>+</sup>-DME increase during the charging process, but the intensity change of Na<sup>+</sup>-DME is more obvious than that of free DME, indicative of more Na<sup>+</sup> coordinated with free DME when the NFM cathode charge, consistent with ex situ FTIR results. Moreover, ex situ FTIR reveals that the P-F stretching of free PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> shifts from 840 cm<sup>−1</sup> to 834 cm<sup>−1</sup>, and the intensity of the peak located at 825 – 798 cm<sup>−1</sup> (corresponding to the P-F stretching of NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt) also gradually increases as the electrolyte concentration rises (upper part of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0003">3a</a>). This suggests the formation of more CIPs/AGGs clusters in high-concentration electrolytes, aligning with NMR results. However, the in-situ FTIR contour map shows that the intensity of CIPs/AGGs clusters significantly intensify only at the end of charging when using the SSAD electrolyte, which reveals the CIPs/AGGs clusters are difficult to maintain during dynamic solvation process due to strong competitive interaction between Na<sup>+</sup>-DME and Na<sup>+</sup>-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0001">1f,h</a>), leading to a CIP/AGGs-poor interface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0003">3b</a>). By comparison, for the WSAS electrolyte, ex situ FTIR shows that the C<span class="icomoon"></span>O<span class="icomoon"></span>C stretching of free DEE is located at 1110 cm<sup>−1</sup>, while the C<span class="icomoon"></span>O<span class="icomoon"></span>C group coordinated to Na<sup>+</sup> has a redshift to 1090 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 1065 cm<sup>−1</sup> for Na<sup>+</sup>-DEE (upper part of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0003">3c</a>). Similar to DME-based electrolytes, the same trend is observed as the salt concentration increases. The in situ FTIR contour map from differential data (lower part of Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0003">3c</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10c,d</a>, Supporting Information) also reveals that Na<sup>+</sup>-DEE exhibits a more significant intensity increase compared to free DEE. But, the CIPs/AGGs clusters from the WSAS electrolyte maintain high intensity throughout cell charging, especially at high voltage, indicating that the CIPs/AGGs clusters are more stable due to a balanced interaction between Na<sup>+</sup>-DEE and Na<sup>+</sup>-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0001">1g,i</a>), resulting in a CIP/AGGs-rich interface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0003">3d</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202410261-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5a0c725c-db4a-43f3-a523-71bc4aad9d4f/adma202410261-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5a0c725c-db4a-43f3-a523-71bc4aad9d4f/adma202410261-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3cc064a6-24ea-4fb2-8f22-1ac0376812ef/adma202410261-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5a0c725c-db4a-43f3-a523-71bc4aad9d4f/adma202410261-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410261-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410261" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Interfacial dynamic evolution and oxidative stability of different electrolytes. In situ FTIR spectra on NFM surfaces in a) SSAD and c) WSAS electrolytes. Note that the ex situ FTIR spectra of different concentrations of electrolytes and pure solvents can effectively help determine changes in peak positions. Schematic of interfacial configuration when using b) SSAD and d) WSAS electrolytes. e) Oxidative stabilities of the SSAD and WSAS electrolytes by the LSV of the Al electrode at a scan rate of 1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. f) Comparison of HOMO energy level with different solvation clusters. The color scheme in Figure f: light purple, Na<sup>+</sup>; orange, P atom; light green, F atom; dark grey, C atom; light grey, H atom; red, O atom; purple and pink, electronic clouds.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To assess the effect of different interfacial states on the oxidative decomposition of electrolytes at high voltage, the anodic behavior of different electrolytes is conducted by LSV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0003">3e</a>). The initial CEI formation potential was approximately 3.5&nbsp;V in the 1st scan for both the WSAS and SSAD electrolytes, indicating a CEI formation process. However, in the 2nd scan, the SSAD electrolyte showed a significant increase in anodic current, suggesting the formation of an unstable CEI in the 1st scan, leading to severe oxidative decomposition. Conversely, the WSAS electrolyte exhibited no significant increase in the 2nd scan, implying the formation of a stable CEI and effective suppression of electrolyte decomposition. In addition, the highest occupied molecular orbital (HOMO) of different solvated clusters is calculated by the density functional theory (DFT) to further analyze the oxidative tendency (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0003">3f</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). More specifically, the HOMO energy level of Na<sup>+</sup>-DME-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup>-DME-2PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> were −8.39&nbsp;eV and −5.21&nbsp;eV, respectively, indicating that increasing PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> in the solvated cluster raises the HOMO level. In comparison, the HOMO level of Na<sup>+</sup>-DEE-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup>-DEE-2PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> were −8.36&nbsp;eV and −5.04&nbsp;eV, respectively, showing that increasing PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> raises the HOMO level more significantly in the WSAS electrolyte. It is well known that a higher HOMO level corresponds to a lower oxidation potential of solvated clusters, which contributes to the preferential formation of a stable interfacial layer, thereby suppressing further oxidative decomposition of the electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0022_R_d57601388e1244" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This tendency reveals that the CIP/AGGs-rich interface, containing a large amount of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> from the WSAS electrolyte, is preferentially oxidized to form a stable CEI, improving the stability of the cathode/electrolyte interface.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410261-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202410261-sec-0060-title">2.4 Interfacial Architecture on 4.0&nbsp;V-Class Cathode</h3>
            
            <p>To understand the interphase composition of CEI, time-of-flight secondary-ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS) with a high surface sensitivity was employed to identify specific chemical components. By normalizing the TOF-SIMS depth profiles to their maximum, the distribution of different fragments is displayed. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0004">4a,b</a>, the NaCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>, NaF<sup>−</sup>, POF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>, and C<sub>2</sub>HO<sup>−</sup> fragments are chosen to represent the decomposition products of electrolytes on the NFM surface. Specifically, the NaCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> fragments primarily originate from solvent decomposition, while NaF<sup>−</sup> and POF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> fragments represent the inorganic components, resulting from the decomposition of NaPF<sub>6</sub> salts, and the C<sub>2</sub>HO<sup>−</sup> fragments are likely products of organic decomposition. For both electrolytes, inorganic components are predominantly located in the surface layer of CEI, whereas organic components are distributed in its inner layer. The inorganic components play an important role in protecting the electrode/electrolyte interface and thereby the 3D rendering images of inorganic products are further visualized to investigate the structure of CEI (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0004">4c</a>). Clearly, the NFM cathode cycled in the SSAD electrolyte with more NaCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> fragments on the outer layer of CEI than that in the WSAS electrolyte, but the distribution of the NaF<sup>−</sup> and POF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> fragments is sparser than in the latter. These results suggest that the solvent of the SSAD electrolyte is more susceptible to oxidation thereby generating the NaCO<sub>3</sub>-species, and forming an uneven, loose CEI, which hinders effective suppression of electrolyte decomposition. In contrast, salt decomposition dominates in WSAS electrolyte, consistent with linear sweep voltammetry (LSV) and theoretical calculations (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0003">3e,f</a>), promoting the formation of a dense, inorganic-rich CEI with improved antioxidative properties at the 4.0&nbsp;V-class cathode surface.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202410261-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f0a170f2-154a-47e9-ac39-11884b2744c3/adma202410261-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f0a170f2-154a-47e9-ac39-11884b2744c3/adma202410261-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/80b7f91a-816d-42a0-8cfc-1e78582f5f55/adma202410261-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f0a170f2-154a-47e9-ac39-11884b2744c3/adma202410261-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410261-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410261" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">CEI characterizations and interfacial structure of the cycled NFM in different electrolytes. TOF-SIMS depth profiles of chemical fragments for CEI using a) SSAD and b) WSAS electrolytes and c) corresponding 3D-mapping images of selected secondary ion fragments. HRTEM images of microstructure on the cycled NFM surface with d) SSAD and e) WSAS electrolytes and the corresponding inverse fast Fourier transformation (IFFT) results for the bulk (I) and surface (II) regions.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Furthermore, the interfacial CEI and microstructure changes of the cycled NFM material were also investigated via high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0004">4d</a> shows that the CEI thickness was 7.7&nbsp;nm for the SSAD electrolyte but only 1.8&nbsp;nm for the WSAS electrolyte, confirming more severe oxidative decomposition of the SSAD electrolyte on the high-voltage cathode surface. Besides, the inverse fast Fourier transformation (IFFT) pattern of local HRTEM images revealed that NFM cycled in SSAD electrolyte only exhibited a layered structure (R-3m)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0023_R_d57601388e1337" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> in the bulk region (I) but an evident cubic structure (Fd3m) in the surface region (II). On the contrary, the NFM cycled in WSAS electrolyte displayed significantly less cubic structure only in the surface region, with the rest remaining layered structure. This implies that the WSAS electrolyte is more compatible with the high-voltage NFM electrode, minimizing the loss of active sites (i.e., the layered structure) and enhancing battery lifespan. The above results are also corroborated by Scanning Electron Microscopy (SEM) and X-ray diffraction (XRD) (Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). It shows that no significant cracking on the cycled NFM surface by the SEM characterization and a smaller shift in the (003) and (104) peak of XRD (representing a layered structure) with the WSAS electrolyte. Together, these findings confirm that the WSAS electrolyte more effectively protects the NFM at high potentials compared to the SSAD electrolyte. Moreover, the corresponding morphology of the counter Na metal electrode is performed by the SEM (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). There is an obvious porous structure for deposited Na when using SSAD electrolyte, highlighting the need for further investigation to understand the differences between the two electrolytes on the anode-free side.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410261-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202410261-sec-0070-title">2.5 Electrochemical and Interfacial Stability upon Na-Plating/Stripping</h3>
            
            <p>To construct a high-energy anode-free battery, the compatibility of electrolytes with sodium metal plated on a Cu current collector also needs to be further investigated. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0005">5a</a>, the interfacial stability and overpotential were evaluated using a Na||Na symmetric cell at 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a cutoff capacity of 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. The overpotential of the cell with Carbonate-based electrolyte exceeded 200&nbsp;mV accompanied by large fluctuations, resulting from the severe reduction decomposition of electrolyte increasing the interfacial impedance. Additionally, although the cell with SSAD electrolyte exhibits a low overpotential of 11.5&nbsp;mV, this stability persisted only for 80&nbsp;h followed by the short circuit. In contrast, the cell with WSAS electrolyte not only shows an ultralow overpotential of 11&nbsp;mV and negligible fluctuation but also maintains a stable interfacial plating/stripping environment for 350&nbsp;h. Besides, the Coulombic efficiency (CE) of the Na||Cu asymmetric cell is also performed at 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a cutoff capacity of 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> with different electrolytes to investigate the Na plating/stripping reversibility (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0005">5b</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). The cycling CE of the cell with Carbonate-based electrolyte is always less than 45% and it could not sustain long-term operation. The cell with SSAD electrolyte shows a high CE at the initial cycling process, but the CE exhibits a massive fluctuation after 100 cycles, indicating unstable Na plating/striping. By comparison, the cell with WSAS electrolyte demonstrated improved and stable long-term plating/stripping performance, with an average CE of 99.89%. The Aurbach test<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0024_R_d57601388e1383" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Energy Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> further confirmed that the WSAS electrolyte had the highest CE of 99.92% (Figure&nbsp;<a class="sectionLink scrollableLink" href="#adma202410261-sec-0090">S16</a>, Supporting Information). Besides, the Na||Cu cell with WSAS electrolyte outperformed the SSAD electrolyte under various low-temperature conditions (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information), which is conducive to improving the performance of AFSMBs in harsh temperatures.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202410261-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/65ddb7ee-2742-453d-a221-0ce117698dad/adma202410261-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/65ddb7ee-2742-453d-a221-0ce117698dad/adma202410261-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/19cee61e-b9bc-4385-ba3b-bc3cafb19212/adma202410261-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/65ddb7ee-2742-453d-a221-0ce117698dad/adma202410261-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410261-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410261" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical plating/stripping reversibility and characterizations of the Na metal plating on Cu in different electrolytes. a) Voltage-time profiles of Na||Na symmetric cells and b) Na plating/stripping Columbic efficiency (CE) of cycling performance in Na||Cu asymmetric cells at 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a capacity of 1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. SEM images of Na plating on the Cu with c) WSAS, (d) SSAD, and e) Carbonate-based electrolytes after 50 cycles. f) C 1s XPS-spectra of SEI on the cycled Cu surfaces form the Na||Cu cells and g) corresponding atomic ratios of different elements in the SEI.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To better understand the difference of Na plating/striping in various electrolytes, the morphology of sodium plated on Cu was examined by SEM. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0005">5c</a>, the plated Na surfaces in Carbonate-based electrolyte was heavily covered with by-products from electrolyte decomposition and dead Na, indicating that the carbonate solvent hinders the formation of a stable SEI on Na metal. The Na plated from the SSAD electrolyte reduced the build-up of side products and dead Na but exhibited an uneven deposition and large cracks with locally prominent sodium dendrites at their edges (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0005">5d</a>). This may be due to the high desolvation energy (as calculated based on the large proportion of solvated clusters, Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information), which creates a high kinetic energy barrier for electrochemical plating. In contrast, Na plated from the WSAS electrolyte shows a uniform, compact, and complete surface, attributed to the formation of a stable SEI (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0005">5e</a>), which alleviates the side reactions originating from highly active Na metal to the electrolyte and reduces the formation of crack during cycling.</p>
            
            <p>The plating behavior of Na metal is closely tied to interfacial properties resulting from electrolyte decomposition. To further investigate this, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was conducted at different etching depths to analyze the SEI on the Na surface. In the C 1s spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0005">5f</a>), the peak intensity of the organic species from the WSAS and SSAD electrolyte, including C<span class="icomoon"></span>O<span class="icomoon"></span>C and C<span class="icomoon"></span>C,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0024_R_d57601388e1440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Energy Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> shows a decreasing tendency as the Ar<sup>+</sup> etching time increases, whereas these peaks remained prominent for the Carbonate-based electrolyte, indicating ongoing solvent decomposition throughout deposition. To further illustrate the component of SEI, a quantitative comparison of elements was performed from the XPS spectra. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0005">5g</a>, the atomic percentage of carbon in the SEI formed from WSAS and SSAD electrolytes decreased with etching time, while the SEI formed in Carbonate-based electrolyte retained a high carbon content, further indicating severe solvent decomposition in the Carbonate-based electrolyte. Meanwhile, the SEI formed in the WSAS and SSAD electrolytes processes higher O and Na contents in the inner SEI, attributed to the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>O-rich species (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0009_R_d57601388e1456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Cent. Sci. Energy Storage Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, both WSAS and SSAD electrolytes had appropriate fluorine content in the outer and inner SEI layers, with the outer layer mainly composed of NaPF<sub>y</sub>/Na<sub>x</sub>PF<sub>y</sub>O<sub>z</sub> and the inner layer mainly composed of NaF (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0025_R_d57601388e1473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Energy">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> contributing to forming a dense SEI. However, the F content distribution in the SEI formed by Carbonate-based electrolyte was uneven. The presence of F species in the inner layer is due to the preferential decomposition of the fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) additive,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0026_R_d57601388e1479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Environ. Sci.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which suppresses solvent reduction during the initial cycling. However, the SEI formed from the Carbonate-based electrolyte is not stable enough, leading to continuous solvent decomposition. As a result, the percentage of C/O species increases, while the proportion of F species decreases in the outer layer. Therefore, the SEI derived from the ether-based electrolytes, especially WSAS electrolyte, exhibited an inorganic gradient structure, with NaPF<sub>y</sub>/Na<sub>x</sub>PF<sub>y</sub>O<sub>z</sub>-rich outer layer and Na<sub>2</sub>O/NaF-rich inner layer, which provided effective Na metal protection and reduced polarization overpotential (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0005">5a</a>). More importantly, the cyclic voltammetry (CV) results of Na||Cu cell with different electrolytes further revealed initial SEI formation potentials of 0.71&nbsp;V for WSAS, 0.69&nbsp;V for SSAD, and 0.46&nbsp;V for Carbonate-based electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information), consistent with the lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) levels (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). This further reveals that anion-participated clusters in the WSAS electrolyte can preferentially reduce to form stable SEI thereby suppressing the electrolyte decomposition.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410261-sec-0080">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202410261-sec-0080-title">2.6 Practical Performance of AFSMBs with WSAS Electrolyte</h3>
            
            <p>To further illustrate the practical viability of WSAS electrolytes, anode-free batteries were assembled (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0006">6a</a>). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0006">6b</a>, the Cu||NFM coin cell with WSAS electrolyte achieved a capacity retention of 82.3% after 100 cycles at 0.5 C, whereas the cell with SSAD electrolyte retained only 48.7% under the same conditions. The cell with Carbonate-based electrolyte failed after just 6 cycles due to severe side reactions, which consumed significant amounts of active sodium and caused a sharp decline in capacity. Meanwhile, to verify the outstanding performance of WSAS electrolyte, the industrial anode-free pouch cells are constructed under harsh conditions with a high capacity of 180 mAh, a lean electrolyte of 4&nbsp;g Ah<sup>−1</sup>, and a multi-layer structure, whose capacity can reach more than 80% until 50 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0006">6c</a>). The anode-free pouch cells exhibited a higher energy density compared to Na-ion batteries of similar capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22a,b</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the calculated energy density of the 10-Ah Cu||NFM pouch cell surpasses that of the Cu||NVP pouch cell (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22c,d</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that the 4.0&nbsp;V-class NFM cathode provides significant advantages in enhancing the energy density of anode-free batteries. In addition, the low-temperature performance of Cu||NFM pouch cell was conducted after charging at room temperature (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information), they delivered 95.5%, 91.6%, 87.2%, 78.4%, and 74.3% of its room temperature capacity at 10&nbsp;°C, 0&nbsp;°C, −10&nbsp;°C, −20&nbsp;°C, and −30&nbsp;°C, respectively, further demonstrate the superior low-temperature applicability of WSAS electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410261-fig-0006">6d</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202410261-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c37575b0-1d35-4211-86e1-7358c1b9fd4a/adma202410261-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c37575b0-1d35-4211-86e1-7358c1b9fd4a/adma202410261-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8c5d5eab-8f22-4f5a-adcf-62115fe70c79/adma202410261-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c37575b0-1d35-4211-86e1-7358c1b9fd4a/adma202410261-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410261-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410261" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical stability of anode-free battery with different electrolytes. a) Schematic construction of an anode-free battery. b) Cycling stability of the anode-free Cu||NFM coin cell using various electrolytes. c) Cycling performance of industrial multi-layer Cu||NFM pouch cells with a capacity of 180 mAh when using the WSAS electrolyte and d) its discharge capacity at low temperatures (from 25&nbsp;°C to −30&nbsp;°C).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202410261-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202410261-sec-0090-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this work, a weakly solvating anion-stabilized (WSAS) electrolyte with robust solvation clusters was designed by inducing anion participation in the solvation process and balancing the interaction between Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent and Na<sup>+</sup>-anion. Using dynamic monitoring via in situ FTIR and refined characterization of CEI composition, we found that the anion-solvated clusters in the bulk environment of WSAS electrolyte remained stable on the high-voltage NFM cathode surface during the dynamic solvation process. This stability facilitated the formation of a CIP/AGGs-rich interface, which contributed to the creation of a uniform and compact inorganic CEI, ultimately improving the cycling performance of the 4.0&nbsp;V-class layered oxide cathode over a wide temperature range from −40 to 60&nbsp;°C. On the anode-free side, the WSAS electrolyte achieved highly reversible Na metal plating/stripping on the bare current collector with an average Coulombic efficiency of 99.89%. This was accomplished by the preferential reduction of the electrolyte to form an inorganic SEI with a gradient structure, consisting of a NaPF<sub>y</sub>/Na<sub>x</sub>PF<sub>y</sub>O<sub>z</sub>-rich outer layer and a Na<sub>2</sub>O/NaF-rich inner layer, which effectively promoted dense deposition of Na meta and suppressed continuous electrolyte decomposition. Deriving from the stable cycling of both Na metal and high-voltage NFM cathode with WSAS electrolyte, the anode-free coin cell retained 82.3% of its initial capacity after 100 cycles, and industrial-class multi-layer anode-free pouch cells with a high capacity of 180 mAh and lean electrolyte of 4.0&nbsp;g Ah<sup>−1</sup>, maintained over 80% capacity retention after 50 cycles and exhibited excellent low-temperature performance (e.g., 74.3% capacity at −30&nbsp;°C). Therefore, our electrolyte solvation engineering opens new directions for developing advanced electrolyte systems, expands the applicability of high-density-energy sodium batteries under harsh conditions, and provides a promising pathway for next-generation rechargeable batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202410261-sec-0100">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202410261-sec-0100-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410261-sec-0110">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410261-sec-0120">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410261-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202410261-sec-0130-title"> Materials</h4>
                  
                  <p>Sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF<sub>6</sub>, battery grade), 1,2-dimethoxyethane (DME, battery grade), and 1, 2-diethoxyethane (DEE, battery grade) were purchased from Duoduo Chemical Co., Ltd. The commercial Carbonate-based electrolyte of 1.0&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolving in PC: EMC (3:7 in volume ratio) + 5&nbsp;wt% FEC (Duoduo Chemical Co. Ltd) was used as a reference. All the above solvents were dehydrated by molecular sieves (4 Å, Sigma-Aldrich). Na[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (NFM) cathode material, Super C65, PVDF binder, and N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP, battery grade) were purchased from Guangdong Canrd New Energy Technology Co., Ltd.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410261-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202410261-sec-0140-title"> Electrolyte Preparation and Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>All the electrolytes were prepared by dissolving 1 or 2&nbsp;M (mol L<sup>−1</sup>, it was calculated based on the moles of salt and the volumes of solvents) of NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DEE or DME with an Ar-filled glove box (the content of O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O levels &lt; 2.0&nbsp;ppm). Specifically, the WSAS and SSAD electrolytes were prepared by dissolving 2&nbsp;M of NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DEE and DME, respectively, and the WSAP and SSSD electrolytes were prepared by dissolving 1.0&nbsp;M of NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DEE and DME, respectively. Conductivity was calculated from the equation: σ = R/(L*S), where R was ohmic impedance from the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) results of the above cells was conducted at a voltage amplitude of 10&nbsp;mV and in a frequency range of 10<sup>6</sup> to 10<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;Hz, and L was the distance between the two stainless-steel plate electrodes (1&nbsp;mm), and S was the area of the gasket center (1 cm<sup>2</sup>). Cell constants were calibrated by a 1.0&nbsp;M KCl in H<sub>2</sub>O solutions. All the curves were performed on Princeton PARSTAT 2000A. The viscosity tests were conducted on a UbbcloHde Viscometer (Shanghai Liangjing Glass Instrument Factory) and the flash point tests were conducted on an automatic Flash Point Tester (DSL-261DZ).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410261-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202410261-sec-0150-title"> Electrode and Battery Preparation</h4>
                  
                  <p>The NFM cathode slurry was prepared by mixing NFM, Super C65, and PVDF with a mass ratio of 85:10:5 in NMP. Then, the uniform NFM cathode slurry was coated on an Al current collector and dried in a vacuum oven at 110&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h. The active mass loadings of the NFM electrode were about 4.5&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The dry anode-free sodium metal pouch cell was provided by CATL. It features a sandwich structure composed of a NFM cathode, a polypropylene (PP) separator, and a Cu current collector, which were then wound into a multi-layer configuration with 5 cathode layers and 6 anode layers. Specifically, the mass loading of the cathode was about 22&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, and the operating voltage from 2.0&nbsp;V to 3.8&nbsp;V. Then the cell was filled with 0.72&nbsp;g pre-prepared electrolytes in the Ar-filled glovebox after drying at 80&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h under vacuum, which was further vacuum wetted for 1&nbsp;min and sealed at −95&nbsp;kPa.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410261-sec-0160">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202410261-sec-0160-title"> Electrochemical Testing</h4>
                  
                  <p>For coin cell configurations, 2032-type cells were assembled using a glass fiber separator (Whatman GF-A) with 100&nbsp;µL electrolyte per cell and tested by a battery test system (Neware). The Na||NFM cells were cycled within a voltage range of 2.0 – 4.0&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) at 1.0 C (1.0 C = 140&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>) after formation under 0.1 C for 2 cycles and the cells were also performed power capacity at different rates (i.e., 1 C, 2 C, 3 C, 4 C, 5 C, 6 C, 7 C, 8 C, 9 C, 10 C). In addition, the high-temperature performance of the Na||NFM cells was tested under 1.0 C at 60&nbsp;°C. For the low-temperature cycling test, the cells were cycled under 0.2 C at 25&nbsp;°C for 2 cycles first and then were cycled under 0.5 C at different temperatures (i.e., 25&nbsp;°C, 10&nbsp;°C, 0&nbsp;°C, −10&nbsp;°C, −20&nbsp;°C, −30&nbsp;°C and −40&nbsp;°C, respectively). For the Aurbach CE measurement, the test protocol was followed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0027_R_d57601388e1673" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (1) the 5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> Na was plated on Cu under 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and then stripped to 1&nbsp;V; (2) plate 5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> Na on Cu again under 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> as a Na reservoir; (3) repeatedly plating/stripping Na of 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> under 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> for 10 cycles; (4) charged to 1.0&nbsp;V to strip all the residual Na. For the cycling Coulombic efficiency (CE) of the Na||Cu asymmetrical cell, the 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> Na was plated on bare Cu foils and stripped to the cut-off voltage of 1.0&nbsp;V at 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, then the CE was calculated by the plated and stripped capacity. For the cyclic voltammetry (CV) and linear sweep voltammetry (LSV) measurements, the Cu and Al foil were used as the working electrode, respectively, where the Na metal was used as the counter and reference electrodes. In addition, the leakage currents of cycled NFM were performed under a constant voltage of 4.0&nbsp;V (versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) after cycling in different electrolytes.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410261-sec-0170">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202410261-sec-0170-title"> Theoretical Simulations</h4>
                  
                  <p>Quantum chemistry calculations were first performed to optimize molecular geometries of DEE and DME solvent molecules using the Gaussian 16 package<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0028_R_d57601388e1707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> at B3LYP/6-311+G(d) level of theory. Two modelling electrolyte systems were constructed and the detailed compositions of the simulation system were listed in Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). The atomic partial charges on these solvent molecules were computed by fitting to the molecular electrostatic potential at atomic centers with the Møller-Plesset second-order perturbation method and the correlation-consistent polarized valence cc-pVTZ(-f) basis set. The atomistic force field parameters for all ions and molecules were described in the AMBER format and were taken from previous work.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0029_R_d57601388e1715" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Phys. Chem. B">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The cross-interaction parameters between different atom types were obtained from the Lorentz-Berthelot combination rule. Atomistic simulations were performed using the GROMACS package with cubic periodic boundary conditions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410261-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410261-bib-0030_R_d57601388e1721" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - SoftwareX">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The equations for the motion of all atoms were integrated using a classic Verlet leapfrog integration algorithm with a time step of 1.0 fs. A cutoff radius of 1.6&nbsp;nm was set for short-range van der Waals interactions and real-space electrostatic interactions. The particle-mesh Ewald (PME) summation method with an interpolation order of 5 and a Fourier grid spacing of 0.15&nbsp;nm was employed to handle long-range electrostatic interactions in reciprocal space. All simulation systems were first energetically minimized using a steepest descent algorithm, and thereafter annealed gradually from 700 K to room temperature (300 K) within 20&nbsp;ns. All annealed simulation systems were equilibrated in an isothermal-isobaric (NPT) ensemble for 20&nbsp;ns of physical time maintained using a Nosé-Hoover thermostat and a Parrinello-Rahman barostat with time coupling constants of 0.4 and 0.2&nbsp;ps, respectively, to control the temperature at 300 K and the pressure at 1&nbsp;bar, respectively. Atomistic simulations were further performed in a canonical ensemble (NVT) for 40&nbsp;ns, and simulation trajectories were recorded at an interval of 100 fs for further structural and dynamical analysis. Representative solvation structures were extracted from extensive atomistic simulations, and these solvation structures were adopted as starting configurations for additional DFT calculations. DFT calculations were performed using the Gaussian 16 software at the same level of theory (B3LYP/6-311+G(d)) and with Grimme's-D3 (gd3bj) dispersion correction to obtain the optimized coordination structures, and thereafter to calculate binding energies and LUMO-HOMO energies of these representative solvation structures.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410261-sec-0180">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202410261-sec-0180-title"> Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>For the electrode characterizations, all the cycled electrodes were first rinsed with pure DME or DEE solvent to remove residual NaPF<sub>6</sub>, dried, and then stored in the glovebox before being transferred for characterizations. The component and element distribution of SEI was acquired by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) (Thermo Scientific ESCALAB Xi+), and all the peaks were fitted based on the reference C1s peak at 284.6&nbsp;eV. The cycled electrode morphology was obtained by Scanning Electron Microscopy (SEM) (Zeiss GeminiSEM 500). The lattice structure and elemental distributions of the cycled NFM materials were performed by high-resolution transmission electron microscope (HRTEM) (Hitachi H-7650). The structure paraments of cycled NFM powders were characterized by powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) (TEVASTAR Y2, Cu-Kα radiation (λ = 1.5405 Å)) from 10° to 80°. In addition, the 3D structure of CEI was characterized by Time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS) (IONTOF M6), the mass spectrometry was equipped with a 30&nbsp;kV Bi<sup>+</sup> analysis beam for depth profiling and a 500&nbsp;eV Cs<sup>+</sup> ion beam was applied for the sputtering of the cycled NFM with a typical sputtered area (100&nbsp;µm × 100&nbsp;µm). For the electrolyte characterizations, the NMR spectra of electrolytes were recorded using a spectrophotometer (Bruker, 500&nbsp;MHz Ultra-ShieldTM) and a mixture of 0.1&nbsp;mol/kg NaPF<sub>6</sub> in 1&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O + 1&nbsp;wt% C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>5</sub>OH + 98&nbsp;wt% D<sub>2</sub>O was used as the NMR internal standard solution. The ex and in situ Fourier-transform infrared (FTIR) spectra were acquired both by the Nicolet 6700 FTIR spectrometer, where a schematic configuration of the FTIR sample cell for electrolyte/electrode interfacial characterizations was shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information). Specifically, the sodium salt or the prepared electrolyte was placed directly on the diamond windows of the FTIR cell for ex situ FTIR to perform the bulk solvation structure, and the dynamic process of cathode/electrolyte interface was conducted by the in situ FTIR characterization.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
